The details of the film have been rehashed by several hundred previous reviewers, so I won't do that once again. I will point out that it is interesting that the intended audience for this film - the baby boomers of 1971 who were then in their teens and twenties who so embraced this film then and still do through all of the websites dedicated to its nostalgia - have become what they claimed to abhor in this movie over the last 35 years. They have, in fact, become the most prudish bunch of old people since the Puritans landed at Plymouth Rock. Their collective slogan has changed from Let It All Hang Out to Let All Who Do Not Conform Be Hanged!. Thanks to the collective legislative efforts of the baby boomers - shortly to begin their second stint in diapers - we do not dare let a 20 year old (Harold's age in the movie) drink one beer even in the bedroom of his own house. We do not dare let bar owners allow smoking in their establishments by anyone no matter what their age, and worst of all the land is dotted with zero-tolerance policies in which a child who shows up to school with an unauthorized aspirin is often treated the same as a child who shows up to school with crack cocaine or a gun as far as expulsion goes.The point of my rant is this - If the Maude of the film were somehow brought back to life today, more than likely she would find Harold acting more like his mother than even his mother did and would likely want to take that handful of pills that ended her life a second time - for completely different reasons.This really is a great film, and I would really hope that when I am 79 I am a cross between the Maude of this movie and Einstein and his compadres in the film IQ. More than that though, I watch this film to remind me how it is a daily battle to stay young at heart and open to the simple pleasures of life. A battle that so many of my older fellow baby boomers have long since lost. "},{"Title":"Black comedy, Unlikely Romance, and Cat Stevens","Date":"9 July 2011","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":" Harold and Maude is one of the original cult classics. A critical and box-office disappointment when released, it has in subsequent years become a classic, if unlikely, romantic comedy.Harold (Bud Cort), who appears to be in his late teens or early 20's, lives in a magnificent mansion with his mother and several servants. He's a quiet fellow, brow-beaten by his (comically) domineering mother, and has a fixation on death his main pleasures come from attending the funerals of strangers (driving himself in his hearse) and from staging imaginative fake suicides to shock his mother.Harold notices that a woman in her 70's is also regularly attending funerals, and she introduces herself Maude (Ruth Gordon, of Rosemary's Baby fame). Maude is a whirlwind of a woman, a genuine free-spirit--she enjoys stealing cars for joy-rides (and as a reminder to car-owners to not get attached to things), she poses nude for an artist, she plays multiple musical instruments, and her home is filled with interesting mementos, keepsakes, and art. Maude is also a font of wisdom--she is a holocaust survivor, she grew up in pre-World War I Vienna and danced at balls at the Palace, she freed animals from cages decades ahead of the animal liberation movement, and she protested and fought for the biggest issues. Maude's wisdom is supplemented by the score by Cat Stevens, who was at the height of his powers as a hippie-dippy mystic able to write profoundly catchy folk-rock songs.Meanwhile, Harold's mother (memorably played by Vivian Pickles), has decided that her son needs to take on adult responsibilities, so has arranged a series of dates for Harold through the services of a computer dating company. Harold, who is becoming smitten with Maude, decides to utilize his experience with fake suicides to horrify and drive away his arranged dates (none of these women are well-matched with Harold since his mother took it upon herself to fill out the application for the dating service). Harold's mother is so incensed that she concludes Harold should be drafted into the army, but Harold and Maude utilize their talents at deception to make that an impossibility.Harold and Maude become lovers, and Harold decides to propose marriage. As alluded to earlier in the film, Maude has other plans--on her 80th birthday, when Harold proposes, she tells him she's taken poison and will be dead by midnight. Apparently, long ago, perhaps in the Camps, she had taken a vow to end her life at 80 if she survived that long, and she kept that vow. Despite Harold's efforts--he calls for an ambulance, Maude is taken to a hospital--she is dead by dawn of the next day. Harold, utterly devastated, drives his customized Jaguar (it looks like a hearse--Harold altered the vehicle after his mother had his previous hearse hauled away) off a cliff. Or so it appears. Though the car goes crashing down, we see that Harold has saved himself--the film ends with him ambling down the road, tentatively playing If You Want to Sing Out, Sing Out on the banjo Maude gave him.Harold and Maude is genuinely hilarious and touching, and Harold's journey from death-obsessed boy to life-affirming man is only made possible because of Maude's profound influence. The irony of Maude's suicide is that she intensely loved life and lived it fully. While many will disagree with her decision to end her own life, no one can say that Maude did not fully embrace life until the very end.The look and sound and much of the content of the film's message was clearly influenced by Summer Of Love-style hippies, so the likelihood you'll be receptive to the film's message is increased if you are 420-friendly (wink), and if you like the music of the 60's and early 70's. It is also a particularly good movie for teenagers--this is a coming-of-age tale, and if you are still trying to figure out the world, or are feeling alienated and alone, then this movie's message can do genuine good. Harold's obsession with death is a clear precursor to Goth, so if you are somewhat gloomy, or are feeling somewhat depressed, Harold and Maude can be a sort of antidote. In the 70ies, Hollywood's audience could be surprised, shocked, made gasp and - most important- ponder over the films by very talented directors and brilliant cast. Harold and Maude is the most characteristic example.It makes us all think not so much of the weird (as some think) plot,twisted characters,but of simple and natural things without which we cannot live,i.e. love and understanding. Cheers to the makers of this timeless cinematic treasure! It soon becomes clear that he is just trying to gets his mother to pay him some attention and show that she loves him, and with this he has already stolen the hearts of the audience. And then there is Maude. How could one not love a 79 year old woman who enjoys stealing cars and lives so carefree? We first meet Maude eating an apple as she watches a funeral. Soon after, we see her steal her first car and we love her for her seize the day lifestyle, because who wouldn't want to live like that? The viewer continually is shown subtle hints to her past life, which allows the viewer to appreciate this attitude all the more. The cut from Harold shooting himself, to yet another funeral scene creates a momentary gasp from the audience. These moments in the movie deepen the emotional connection the viewer has with the characters. Every time Harold fakes a suicide, the cinematography allows the viewer to think, just for a moment and although they know it is not true, that Harold may have actually killed himself. The directors and editors use this to their advantage until the very last scene, making a remarkably edited film. The eccentric, but lovable characters and the terrific art used in making the film allows the viewer to completely forget that the plot is absolutely ridiculous. Because we have an immediate love for the characters, it doesn't bother us that Harold is always attempting completely unbelievable suicides and because of the editing, we almost believe them. Maude's attitude toward life makes us love her and we are completely entertained by all the ridiculous stuff she does and as the viewer gets more and more subtle hints as to what her past as consisted of, our respect and love just keeps growing. This movie is a must see, if not for the fall-out-of-your-seat laughter, than for the artful editing. These quick facial signs show he wants to live after all. Also, in multiple places, his face shows a deep admiration and love for Maude. This also is strange because she is almost eighty while he is nineteen. Ruth Gordon portrays Maude's fearless sense of adventure and will to lives everyday to the fullest, very well. She even sings remarkably well for an eighty year old. Without the skilled acting of Bud Cort and Ruth Gordon as Harold and Maude, the movie would not be the classic that it is. The next scene is Harold engulfed in flames. Following this, the girl runs out screaming while Harold casually walks in from the back and smiles at the camera. The ultimate letting go for Harold comes at the end of the movie when Maude does something very surprising, (although there are clues if you look for them) that forces Harold to let go of the intense love he has for her. Letting go and living life moment by moment is a lifelong lesson presented in Harold and Maude. Harold and Maude is an excellent movie that combines skilled acting, crafty cinematography and a unique story line. Looking deeper than the physical aspects of the film lies the artistic beauty that can be found in the contrasts of two opposite people. In this story the underdog, Harold, comes out a changed and better man in the end of the movie. This story line appeals to many viewers whether or not they can directly relate to his situation.The movie was filmed in the San Francisco Bay area and released in America in 1971. The film was directed by Hal Ashby and written Colin Higgins, with featured music by Cat Stevens throughout. Although the film was a box office disappointment, it has since reached new heights, being praised by film critics and avid-viewers alike as a funny, believable, charming movie.Harold's character, played by Bud Cort, is a young man coming from a rich family who goes against his mother's every will in a desperate attempt to gain her attention and affection. He continually stages his own deaths and suicides, some more ridiculous than others, until she reaches her wits end and orders him to marry in an attempt to fix his attitude for good. Unlike most young men his age, Harold enjoys visiting funerals, graveyards, and demolition sites. Although he doesn't find a bride his mother would approve of at any of his hangouts, he does find a new and wonderful friend.Maude's character is the pivotal person in Harold's life. Like him, she enjoys the morbid, but for different reasons. Through her eccentricities and unconditional love for living things, she shows Harold that life can be good if it's truly enjoyed and appreciated to the fullest each day. A lot of people enjoy being dead, but they're not dead. They're just backing away from life. Maude is considerably older than Harold, and seems to take him under her wing through the movie, helping him find things in life that make him happy besides being dead.The culmination of this happy-go-lucky movie is Harold and Maude falling in love, or Harold falling in love with her. He shows her the things that he has learned in a birthday party he throws for her 80th birthday. All is going well until Harold discovers she has poisoned herself in order to die because she doesn't believe she wants to live beyond 80. Harold is distraught and takes her to the hospital, but not before it's too late. The audience can sympathize with his sadness and grief at the loss of what we believe is his only true friend in life, and his lover.The life lessons Maude taught Harold are obvious at the end of the movie, when the shot angles and scenes lead us to believe he will drive himself off a cliff in one last suicide, but walks away from the cliff with a banjo in his arms, plucking out If You Want to Sing Out, Sing Out by Cat Stevens. He is going to go on and live life to the fullest, so he can truly live before he dies.The film is full of interesting shots and scenes. There are some strange angles and locations that keep the viewer intrigued and following the story. A great scene that allows the viewer to think that they are really watching the couple as they evolve as people is the scene where they are dancing in Maude's house. The camera pans out through the window and tracks them at their eye level as they dance down her streetcar home to a Cat Stevens song. This is just one example of the brilliant filming abilities and directing used in the film.The film also uses the music of Cat Stevens to illustrate scenes in a new light. The scene where Harold and Maude are in the military graveyard is one example of this. As the camera view pans out and up to show more and more of the massive graveyard, the song Where do the Children Play cries out above the dark scene. This seems to be a reference not only to wars in general but possibly the Vietnam War specifically. The scene is just one political statement made in the movie, but one that stood out strongly to me.The movie starts out very strange, and very slow. The viewer isn't given much information to go on or use to enjoy the film fully until Maude appears and shakes up the structure of Harold's life. The ending of the movie is the culmination of a warm and exciting journey that the audience has followed Harold make and felt as though they were apart of, and the way he handles Maude's death is a closing scene that is sure to stick on minds and hearts of all kinds of viewers. "},{"Title":"a romantic black comedy of the most light-hearted/sinister psychological ideals","Date":"10 December 2006","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":" I wouldn't say I really clicked into Harold and Maude entirely on a first viewing- maybe I may never will. It almost has something to it at times that I found a bit unique for that un-trustworthy brand of cinema known as the romantic comedy. It's not the last time it's happened, but what I found fascinating about the psychological interest the character of Harold (Bud Cort, dead-pan to the point of inertia sometimes), is that we see really what is going on inside Harold's head in the midst of the actual scene as it's playing out, subjectivity in the guise of objective, cold shooting. The best example(s) can be found when he tries to commit suicide- more than once as it were. It's actually kind of manipulative, and if you've ever known someone or even been a survivor of suicide it might be very off-putting to see it put on such bizarre display here. But it adds to the perverse fun of the picture to see that hopelessness is really the only thing from keeping these fantasies going on as Harold's head-strong, perpetually bourgeois mother (Vivian Pickles, a perfectly one-track performance), keeps trying to set him up with women...or else it's off to the army. It's a creative cinematic trick, though it might have even worked itself greater in a short film, or even as an actual novel where Harold (and Maude for that matter) could be revealed even more with their psychological insides and emotional bearings and depths.So in a sense Harold and Maude only sometimes gets under the surface. But said surface ends up making for the kind of unusual movie that doesn't give 'unusual' a bad name at all. It was the second feature directed by Hal Ashby, and his acute sense of the visual with the purity of either eccentric/bottled-up people, or just stiff rich folks, is uncanny. His strengths at keeping Colin Higgins rocky, predictably unpredictable screenplay in check is also a plus. Yet Harold and Maude, for all of its successes at making some uproarious comedic moments as Harold keeps himself systematically detached not just from his mother, psychiatrist, and crazy army-lifer uncle, but from the proposed ladies as well (there is one, the last one, who is an actress who actually 'gets' what Harold is doing with a Kari-Kiri ritual, is maybe the funniest of all), doesn't do as much as I thought it might in really getting underneath this young man and old woman. There's an interesting split of the two sides of life- despair and joy- that make up the sort of existential mishaps both characters get into, and likely draw one another to each other as mutual funeral crashers. If only it wasn't so quirky all the time- sometimes too much whimsy can backfire.Still, the bit of unease I felt from the film's mis-fires in its broad strokes at black comedy (my least favorite being the one with Harold, the uncle, and Maude as a protester on the rocky cliffs), there was enough that was attained by the actors to make it worthwhile and at least worth one viewing. In fact, I'd say I would watch this again just for Ruth Gordon, who we learn just from one quick shot (a tattoo on her arm, don't miss it) about why perhaps she is who she is at this age, and it's maybe too quick yet crucial to understanding who she is. She is, on the surface, a kind of overly bright, near-crazy fire-fly burning on into that crossroads of 79 into 80. She also is a wonderful contrast to Cort, who along with his dead-pan qualities down pat, can also have fun too in doing his weird little suicidal moments (I loved the bit in the bathtub), or when he has to get more emotional revealing his first brush with death. In the end, I'm reminded of a line from Scorsese's the Departed 'death is easy- life is much harder'. We may never know if Harold becomes joyful, especially with such a sad ending that ends up coming, but there is a slight skip to his step as he plays the banjo, which is a kind of catharsis from seeing this glimpse into the lives of these two 'made-for-each-other' characters.Harold and Maude, with its sweet and puffy folk songs by Cat Stevens, and fine cinematography and editing, is a very good effort at stretching what is expected out of the dreaded 'rom-com', by giving something completely different and still sticking to a template of love conquering all...in a manner of speaking. Not Ashby's best, but far from his worst either. 7.5/10 "},{"Title":"35th anniversary of a classic","Date":"12 November 2006","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":" Turner Classic Movies is currently showing this, perhaps due to the 35th anniversary, perhaps not. "},{"Title":"Still Quirky and Funny Black Comedy Tells the Ultimate Opposites-Attracts Love Story","Date":"11 September 2006","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":" This 1971 black comedy is still a stitch, even if it doesn't feel as audacious as I'm sure it once did. Directed by the maverick Hal Ashby, it is the epitome of all opposites-attract love stories as a twenty-year old depressive connects with a 79-year old life-loving woman. They actually bond over their mutual interest in death, as they keep showing up at the same funerals. However, the root of their respective interests couldn't be more different - Maude shows up to reconfirm how lucky she feels to be alive, while the hearse-driving Harold simply likes the morbidity of it all.The plot is really about their burgeoning relationship, while his mother constantly attempts to make the death-obsessed Harold more socially acceptable. First, she uselessly sets him up on a series of blind dates. To foil any hope of romance with these women, however, he stages mock-suicides which simply perturbs rather than disturbs his mother. She then recruits an ineffectual analyst and even has her brother Victor, a hawkish officer and Vietnam War amputee, to try to convince Harold to join the Army. The episodic structure of the story by Collin Higgins (years before becoming a director in his own right with Foul Play and Nine to Five) allows the characters to develop in subtle ways with a completely deadpan approach despite the outward shenanigans of the characters' actions. The movie eventually moves toward a greater gravitas but despite some soap opera elements, surprisingly not at the expense of the pervasive whimsical tone.Ruth Gordon is her typically pixilated self, an archetype she played for years afterward, while Bud Cort is terrific in conveying his particular brand of wide-eyed cynicism. It's really their unforced chemistry that keeps the film feeling fresh 35 years later. There are funny sideline performances by Vivian Pickles as Harold's eye-rolling mother, Ellen Geer as an overly actressy blind date named Sunshine, and Eric Christmas in a very funny cameo as a disapproving priest. Cat Stevens' soundtrack hasn't aged as well as, say, Simon and Garfunkel's music in The Graduate, but it still works as a folkish commentary on the quirky proceedings. Unfortunately, the 2000 DVD contains a rather compromised print and there are no extras included. It's Hal Ashby's quirky black comedy about a strange rich boy named Harold, played by bug-eyed Bud Cort, who is so bored he stages trick suicides to annoy his uncaring mother. Harold meets octogenarian Maude (Ruth Gordon) an eccentric who loves life. She helps Harold out of his depression and teaches him to love life again, as well as a few other things. The theme of a May-December romance was quite novel in its time. The soundtrack is by folk singer/songwriter Cat Stevens and features many of his most popular tunes. The movie is a minor film but has its own charm and an uplifting message. "},{"Title":"An eccentric, quirky story of discovering life and love...","Date":"10 October 2004","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Bored and desperate for love and attention, young Harold dwells in his own misery and tries to bring everyone around him down along with him. Obsessed with death, his morbid personality is a reflection of his dissociation with the world around him. His rich mother simultaneously ignores him and smothers him, leaving Harold starved for affection and longing to join his deceased father, yet lacking the conviction to actually end his miserable existence.Enter Maude, a totally over-the-top senior citizen who is loopy, lovable, and overflowing with life. They form a unique bond and develop a relationship which changes Harold profoundly.This movie stands the test of time. While the cars and clothes may be dated, the characters express feeling and emotion which does not change over time. And while all this sounds very deep and dramatic, this is actually one of the funniest movies I've ever seen. You really have to see this one for yourself, and I'm happy to hear that new generations of film lovers are continuing to enjoy this cult favorite. "},{"Title":"CK's version explain few ways that dramatic and cinematic elements help advance the plot","Date":"25 April 2004","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Harold and Maude directed by Hal Ashby is by far one of the best movies I've ever seen.  It is full of humor, many hysterical puns, and many creative references to death that just seem to add to the humor greatly. The camera angles seemed to be set perfectly to catch the exact desired effect the director was looking for; such as in the beginning of the movie the camera is placed in a way, so that you only see Harold's lower half right up to the point where he hangs himself Leaving you in suspense as to what has just happened.  The lighting in this particular scene also helped to set the mood. It was rather dark up until the point of the hanging where back lighting from the window behind Harold seemingly show cases his hanging body.  The odd thing about this scene at first though is that the music sounds really up beat, and you wouldn't think Harold was getting ready to hang himself.  This in actuality is one of the clever things the director added to the film.  You find out later on that Harold enjoys death, so why not have an uplifting song play just before Harold does something that he loves.  It's pure genius I think. I also believe that the actors in the movie were chosen wonderfully to fit their character.  Harold for instance played by Bud Cort actually looks like someone who would have this slightly dark or morbid sense of humor.  He had dark hair and dark eyes and wore a lot of dark clothing.  His monotonous voice also added to his character because it would be a little less effective to have some happy go lucky person trying to get the point across that they love death.  Maude played by Ruth Gordon was also a great pick to play the part of a spicy, little old lady. She just seemed to epitomize the older version of every 8 year old girl in America and she did it well. The way she scurried around on camera, the smile that was always on her face, even right down to the curiosity. She always seemed to have this look about her that just fit perfectly.  Hal Ashby definitely knew what he was doing when he chose these two actors for the roles of Harold and Maude. I don't think he could have cast anyone who would have done a better job at fitting these characters and carrying out the plot so well.  "},{"Title":"What Helps the Plot Along","Date":"25 April 2004","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Harold and Maud's main asset is its interesting plot and use of dark humor. Though it is mainly driven by its original premise and compelling plot, there are many dramatic and a few cinematic elements used to aid in character development and setting establishment. The plot was advanced immensely by being able to watch the characters interact with each other. Maud's carefree and childlike expression and joyous motions make her a more believable love interest for her young lover. Harold's deadpan expression and body movement help us to understand his bizarre character as well as add humor to the movie. One terrific example is Maud's body language when Harold finds her modeling for an artist at her house. Her expression indicates that she is not taking herself particularly seriously, but her body moves with a grace that denies her age. The way Harold looks at her at this point marks the beginning of their strange romance, one that would be hard to comprehend if not for the characters developed by the dramatic elements of the film. The setting added a huge amount to the effect of the movie. The field of daises added to Maud's speech about individualism and the rows of gravestones gave a shocking illustration to the movies theme of the eventual mortality that everyone faces. The dark lighting during the scene where Harold professes his love for Maud provides foreshadowing of Maud's imminent death. The harsh lighting cast on Harold's mother makes her into a rather humorous figure by portraying her as rather militant. The dramatic elements add the most to the humorous aspects of the movie. Harold's fiercely military uncle would not be nearly as funny if we were not able to see him pull on a string to draw his missing limb into a salute. Harold's fake suicides certainly would not be as funny if we weren't able to see his mother's utter indifference (or just plain frustration) every time she finds him covered in blood, lying in the most inconvenient places. If it wasn't balanced out by funny images and expressions used by the actors, Harold and Maud would be downright depressing. The dramatic elements swing the movie from the drama genre to a superb and subtle dark comedy. The beautiful natural settings (and not to mention the wonderfully appropriate music) also relieve the darkness of the movie. "},{"Title":"Great, but not for everyone","Date":"4 March 2004","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  What can go wrong in a film in which a suicidal 17 year old boy falls in love with a free-spirited 85 year old woman?  Surprisingly, not much. Directed by Hal Ashby, Harold and Maude puts a spin on the traditional coming-of-age story and turns a seemingly taboo relationship into something that the audience can actually swallow.  There are still some uncomfortable moments, like when Harold and Maude are laying in bed together, but overall Ashby was able to eliminate most of the discomfort surrounding such a typically unacceptable relationship. The film begins with one of Harold's many staged suicide attempts, and it sets the mood for the entire film morbidly humorous and incredibly odd. You find yourself laughing in places you think you shouldn't and caring for characters you never thought you could care for.  As the film progresses and Maude eases Harold out of his shell, the age gap between the characters seems to shrink.  The relationship and its evolution create an interesting and evocative story that keeps you entertained through the entire film. It is important to keep in mind that not everyone will like this film. Those who gravitate towards traditional romantic comedies and mostly realistic love stories will probably want to steer clear of Harold and Maude.  Much of the film functions on an absurd level, like Harold's early relationship with his mother, and the series of dates that his mother sets up for him.  But, anyone who enjoys black humor and inventive storylines will probably be satisfied. It gets mentioned several times in the movie. The draft was inescapable for the majority of young Americans at that time, which makes his uncle's diatrabes about the glory of Army life extremely ironic. "},{"Title":"A celebration of life and the eccentricities that make every person unique.","Date":"19 May 2003","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  In life, people are often faced with circumstances that can leave them changed, or lead to eccentric behavior. This is the case of Harold (Bud Cort) in the film, `Harold and Maude.' Known as a cult classic, `Harold and Maude' is a film that transcends its time with a daring love story between a young man between the ages of 18-20 and an elderly woman nearing the age of 80. The film is reminiscent of the story of `Auntie Mame' in that despite its dark comic approach, `Harold and Maude' is in fact a celebration of life and the eccentricities that make every person unique.  	The story follows that of young Harold (Cort), a rich teen who constantly fakes suicide perhaps to gather attention from his mother, a shallow socialite who has no comprehension of her son's afflictions. One of Harold's favorite past times is to attend funerals, where in fact he meets Maude (Ruth Gordon), an elderly woman whose peculiar behavior leads her to steal cars, among other things, and to appreciate life beyond what the average person does. The two meet at a wake and become close friends. Maude teaches Harold the importance of life beyond the norms by seeing the beauty and intrigue that exists and every object that inhabits this earth. The two generate a connection that ultimately leads to love, and makes Harold question his own wants in life with a family and society that are too conventional for his tastes.  	 `Harold and Maude' isn't just a love story; it's a dark comedy. One can't help but laugh how Harold's phony suicides are portrayed. From stringing himself up in a noose to pretending to chop his arm off, Harold thinks of anything that will shock and disturb his mother. But to his chagrin, nothing works. What's more, the actions of the unusual Maude of stealing cars and fooling police officers, it will leave anyone giggling.  	Aside from its whimsical nature, `Harold and Maude' is relatively bleak as it deals with issues such as death and suicide. Similar issues were raised by a recent theatrical release, `The Hours,' which followed the story of three different women, in three different time periods who were all profoundly affected by Virginia Woolf's novel, `Mrs. Dalloway.' In one scene of `The Hours,' Woolf's husband asks her why someone has to die in her book. Her answer, `Someone should die in order that the rest of us appreciate life.' That's basically the message that one receives from `Harold and Maude,' as well. Death is a part of life although upsetting and sometimes unfair, it happens. Ergo, perhaps people should gain a greater appreciation for their own life when someone close to them dies.  	All in all, `Harold and Maude' is a delightful treasure among other films in the romantic comedy genre. It's begs the question of love existing between two very different people who have so much in common that to avoid love would be a mistake. The film is available for rental at local video stores and for purchase. ***1/2  "},{"Title":"Everything you've heard about this wonderful film is TRUE!!","Date":"16 April 2003","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Hal Ashby's masterpiece still resonates with charm, warmth, absurdity and one of the best soundtracks in movie history. "},{"Title":"Anti-VietNam War Classic","Date":"11 September 1999","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  This film, though unappreciated for its true theme, is still a classic today. Colin Higgins wrote the screenplay as an undergraduate--as a lark, and was flatly told it was a flop. It was finally shot during the height of the Vietnam War. When it appeared, it was received by the Woodstock generation as an anti-war film. The romance between Harold and Maude is incidental (but definitely the break point for viewers). One must look beneath to see Higgin's focus. If you enjoy it, count yourself lucky that you can see deep meaning where others see disgust. Ruth Gordon said she enjoyed making this film more than all her others. "},{"Title":"still holds up well after 1/4 century","Date":"24 November 1998","Score":"","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Despite its '60's sensibilities and flower-power overtones, this is an outrageously funny (and touching) movie that still holds its own after nearly 3 decades. When it was first released by Paramount during the Christmas season of 1971, it was met with scathing reviews--and largely ignored. It was quickly discovered  by college students though, and continues to be a cult classic--particularly among college students. A surprising number of people (including myself) list this among their favorite movies, if not their *favorite* movie...how many other movies released so long ago can make such a claim? It's certainly different 20 year old death-obsessed boy meets and falls in love with new-age octogenarian. It's not tasteless though. This film looks beyond the surface and there is great beauty in their doomed relationship. Cort and Gordon here complete each other--each has something the other desperately needs. Many of the lines are priceless--and immensely quotable. This is probably part of the reason it's achieved staying power. The dialogue is still as fresh and witty as it was in 1971--and Cort's fake suicides are truly hilarious. So is Vivian Pickles as his mother. Things wrap up a little too quickly--and the ending is ambiguous--but I think this was intended. Like a great painting, its meaning is open to interpretation, and asks more questions than it answers. A movie that begs to be seen more than once to fully appreciate. The underrated Bud Cort and late Ruth Gordon have an on-screen chemistry that transcends their ages--and just blows you away. That is, they think of the most unlikely things they can think of co-existing together, and then say to themselves, What if x, and then y, and then z happened? What would happen then? How could I tell that story? And that is what it seems happened here. What if you took a cloistered, depressed, suicidal 20-year-old male who believes that he has nothing to look forward to in life, nothing to live for, and then hooked him up with a 79 year-old woman, a woman full of vim and vinegar and zest for life, and who shows him how to live? And what if theirs then ends up as a romantic --sexual-- relationship? What are the various ironies and paradoxes and oddities that could be explored? What kinds of things could that story tell us? Where would these people end up in the end?I saw this movie back in 1971 when it came out, and found it quite funny.But, I gotta say, I thought then that the idea of a sexual relationship between a 20-year-old male and a 79-year-old woman was very strange, even borderline sicko, and now, 34 years later, I still do. I know that's a big part of the point (i.e., exploring the unusualness of that mating), but, sorry, I can't get past it. "},{"Title":"An E-type hearse and a zest for life","Date":"29 August 2004","Score":"1 out of 2 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Harold and Maude rates highly in any film connoisseur's list as, perhaps, the black comedy of all time. The film was released in 1971 and has a superb cast, lead by Ruth Gordon (who won and Oscar for her role in Rosemary's Baby in 1968) as the 79-year-old Maude, and Bud Cort (Romero in Coyote Ugly) as the 20-year-old Harold.Harold is a rich kid, who craves attention from his mother, but only gets it when he fakes his own suicide. Discovering the power of death, his curiosity takes him to the funeral of someone he doesn't know. His fascination leads him to more funerals, where he sees Maude and realises that she, too, is an uninvited guest.Harold doesn't have much of a life; he has left school, but doesn't know what to do with his future. His mother lives in a social whirl and isn't very interested in him &#150; apart from her aim to find him a wife and get him off her hands.Maude is a dear old lady, with real vitality and a zest for life; she wants to cram in as much as she can before the Grim Reaper knocks on her door! From the day they meet, Harold and Maude become inseparable. She has so much to teach him about life, and he has so much to learn.On his journey of self-knowledge, Maude helps him as no person ever has done before. The film has some excellent humour and comedy lines, bordering on slapstick that hit the nail on the head every time.As the relationship between these two eccentric souls strengthens, Harold's personality and self-respect grows, whilst his mother, even more worried by his affection for an old lady, goes to all lengths to save him &#150; even buying him an E-Type Jaguar (which he converts into an E-Type hearse).This black comedy is not all black. It has a certain feel-good factor about it and it is incredibly funny. There are elements of suspense, romance and intrigue, as well as a large portion of satire.The opening sequence will make you wonder if you really want to watch the rest of the film, but after the first few minutes you will not feel so uncomfortable and will be gripped by the storyline that will not release you until the credits come up at the end. And what an end it is!Ironically, the soundtrack music is by Cat Stevens; surprisingly, it doesn't feel out of place, and it helps to bring the 70's ambiance to the screen.There are so few films around these days that do not insult your intelligence, if you get a chance to see Harold and Maude you really should. And if you can, take a very close friend so that you are both guaranteed a shoulder to cry on. Eraser Head is another weird one, but Eraser Head better qualifies as horror, where it is very hard to place this movie. It has an ominous overtone that runs through the whole movie and yet there are many light and uplifting moments as well.Harold is an adolescent boy who is living a wretched and stultifying life in an American upper class family. They live in a castle. His life is run by his mother who is very nearly completely ignorant of his real existence. He struggles to get her attention by performing very realistic and violent suicide attempts. He amuses himself by going to funerals where he bumps into the ancient and flaky old lady Maude. Maude has been around a little bit and has managed to craft a life every bit as eccentric as Harold himself, but with considerably more happiness. She lives in an old passenger rail car. They begin to have some adventures together and what finally happens is a grim confrontation with Harold's obsession with death.What makes the movie so strange is its very casual relationship with the real world. Scenes have an occasionally lucid hallucinogenic quality to them. It strays into odd counter-culture themes with no political orientation. The characters are stark and graphic. I borrowed it recently from the library on DVD and was also glad to see scenes from the Northern California Bay Area that are now gone forever.Background music is rather perfectly provided by Cat Stevens. "},{"Title":"elements","Date":"24 April 2004","Score":"1 out of 2 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  In Harold and Maude, an untraditional love story between a very young man and an eighty-year-old woman, the cinematic and dramatic elements certainly aid in bringing out the characters, setting, plot, and theme of the film.Although there were no outstanding special effects in this movie, the cinematic elements definitely advance the plot.  An example is that the mood at the beginning of the movie was set due to the fact that the camera focused on Harold's feet and legs, building up suspense until we finally see that he has `hung' himself. The character of Harold is unique because he is set-apart from most boys his age.  Instead of being obsessed with nice cars and parties, Harold enjoys driving his hearse to the funerals of people he doesn't even know. The dramatization of Harold is really what makes him who he is.  His facial expressions seem to be speaking more than his words.  For example, the way that Harold looks at his mother makes his disgust for her very obvious. Without these facial expressions, Harold just wouldn't be the character he is. The part of Harold's mother was also enhanced with dramatization.  The way she carried herself and the way she spoke really gave the audience a clear representation of her elite status.  Her wardrobe also reflects her luxurious lifestyle with fancy dresses and hats. Maude, a free-spirited old woman who defies all stereotypes of an elderly woman, wants to live her life to the fullest, especially in her last week. She doesn't let anything hold her back.  Maude's nature is reflected in both her costume and her acting.  The fact that she wears light and airy colors to funerals shows us that she doesn't participate in anything that is considered proper or appropriate.  Maude can not understand the reasoning behind doing things just because they are expected of you.  She doesn't view death as a sad thing, but as just part of life.  She makes this clear when she says that eighty-years-old is a `good time to pass on.' Harold and Maude's relationship with each other is shown partly through their eyes, especially through Harold.  His affection for Maude is obvious by the way he looks at her.  I believe that his love would be obvious even if he never said a word. Perhaps a theme of this movie is that love has no boundaries.  This theme is brought out by both conversations and the interaction between Harold and Maude.  For example, Harold says `I like you Maude.'  Right after these words, he and Maude are shown dancing.  This scene is one of the parts in the film that shows both the contrast between the two ages, and the closeness that they share. She 'borrows' things from others, to 'remind them that stuff is here today, gone tomorrow.' Sometimes they have fun just by going down to the salvage yard and watch the operation.Mom gives Harold a new car, a Jaguar sportster. He takes a blowtorch, and presumably lots of other tools, and makes it look like a hearse sportscar! Harold announces to his mom that he is getting married, to Maude. Harold surprises Maude with a party for her 80th birthday. However, Maude says she has to be going soon -- she already took the pills. Rushed to the hospital, they try to save her but can't.  Harold weeps, drives his cars to the cliffs and sends it over, crashing to the beach below. "},{"Title":"Harold's Journey to Salvation","Date":"21 June 2004","Score":"2 out of 4 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Harold and Maude is a profoundly spiritual film. It is a story of a death-obsessed 22-year-old neurotic Harold and an eccentric, touchingly childish 80-year-old sorceress Maude. The whole movie is seen through the prism of Harold's internal struggles. He is hungry for life, yet is mortified by what he perceives its utter lack of meaning. He wants sympathy, affection and attention, and tries to attain all of them by means of committing fake suicides -- but all for naught Harold's ice-cold and haughty mother ignores him, ignores him as a sovereign human being, while treating him as a domesticated pet. At a certain juncture, Harold meets a highly non-conformist and easy-going old lady, Maude. She is sent to poor Harold by the dint of fate in order to rescue his soul from the utter morbidity within which it had sunk. The relationship which develops between them is beautifully portrayed in the film. Maude is conjured up into existence by Harold's guardian angel. She heals him. She cures him. She shares with him her internal sources of vitality. She teaches him the science of happiness, the most difficult science to master. She gives her life to Harold. The film leaves you with a lingering bitter-sweet feeling. Harold and Maude is a philosophical elegy, a film of lasting value, a movie the importance of which is only bound to increase. Wonderful work of art. "},{"Title":"An idealistic post-Graduate story","Date":"7 August 2005","Score":"0 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":" This movie was made four years after The Graduate, which has now become much more popular and infamous than this film. The subject matter is similar. Like that film, Harold and Maude focuses on a disaffected young man from a wealthy family who develops a relationship with an older woman. One scene in particular--where Harold is shown in a pool, underwater, in one of his fake suicides--seems directly reminiscent of a scene in The Graduate. Yet here is where the similarities seem to end.While the relationship between Hoffman's Benjamin and Anne Bancroft's Mrs. Robinson is purely sexual, Harold actually falls in love with Ruth Gordon's lively, optimistic Maude, whose passion and vitality are the antithesis of everything in Harold's sheltered, spoiled life. As a result, while The Graduate seems more of an exploration of the cynical, disillusioned elements of the late 60s, Harold and Maude rejects these ideas in favor of a more poignant, heartfelt story. Harold's romantic/sexual attachment to Maude is incidental; it is more important that Harold learns how to live life day by day, to embrace different experiences, and, most importantly, to love.And while this message today may seem dated, it's not one we hear nearly often enough today. The movie also transcends its message with memorable, likable characters Harold, a rich, lonely twenty-year-old who frequents funerals and feels that fake suicides are the only way he can get the attention of his inattentive, self-absorbed mother, and Maude, a vital, lively octogenarian who borrows cars, poses nude for ice sculptures, and smokes bongs in her spare time. Their attachment to one another is genuine, and in spite of the ick factor of their age difference you find yourself rooting for the characters.Harold's faked suicide attempts can be a little disturbing for the faint of heart, but if you can get through the gore of some they turn out to be some of the funniest scenes in the movie. This is one of my favorites. With its unique characters and upbeat message, it is a movie that is about being happy and loving life, something that many films of subsequent decades seem to have lost sight of. Anderson films explore eccentric characters who are often stoic and their reactions to life's events. Harold is such a character and his actions remind me greatly of those of Luke Wilson's character Ritchie Tenenbaum. Furthermore, the manner in which Ashby conveys his character's emotions to his audience must have influenced Anderson. Anderson often places emotional scenes against a background of folk music, which is reminiscent of the entirely Cat Stevens soundtrack that Ashby uses to keep his story clean and simple. I have long been an Anderson fan. I am glad to have found a film that I feel must have served to influence Anderson's unique style. I am also intrigued that Bud Cort is, in fact, a character in The Aquatic Life of Steve Zissou. This connection seems to enforce the idea that Anderson was impressed by Harold and Maude. However, while the movie does possess many unique qualities, its best quality is how the dramatic and cinematic elements work together to bring out the literary elements. This movie has some of the best use of zoom in to show the slightest change in facial expressions.  For example, after Harold sets the dummy on fire to scare off yet another date, the camera zooms in and shows a slight smirk on his face, and while the smirk could be described in a novel, the combination of the zoom in and the actor convey a subtle character change in Harold that could not be accurately conveyed by words.  Another part of the cinematic elements that works to better bring out the characters is the use of the edit and cut.  For instance, after Harold reveals to his mother that he is going to marry Maude, there are several cuts where various people, like Harold's psychiatrist, a priest, and others express their opinions about the matter, and a novel would not be able to do that without causing too much confusion to the reader.  A weakness of the novel is the fact that an author cannot jump from time to time without confusing the reader of implementing literary elements like a flashback, but a movie can jump from time to time easily, without having to worry about confusing the viewer. Not only did cinematic elements work to bring out the literary elements, but dramatic elements also helped as well.  Costumes can be very helpful in the process of characterization, and costumes were definitely used to their fullest extent in the film.  Maude is always in seemingly random clothes, which show her free spirit and her accepting personality, while Harold's costume seem to mimic the costumes of those around him, like the psychiatrist, and that shows how he does not exactly know how to act. Also, the use of props greatly helped the process of characterization as well. Maude's house is full of various and sundry things, which shows her eccentric personality.  Harold's house, on the other hand, has what his mother wants it to have, and shows the viewer that his mother is trying to control every aspect of his life. Without the use of cinematic and dramatic elements, the literary elements would not be understood or appreciated.  While some may prefer cinematic to dramatic, or dramatic to literary, or even literary to the previous two, it is my opinion that this film, Harold and Maude, shows how wonderful the product can be if all three complement each other.  "},{"Title":"dramatic and cinematic elements","Date":"25 April 2004","Score":"0 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  The dramatic and cinematic elements of Harold and Maude bring out characterization. Dramatic and cinematic elements show characterization through actions, clothes, and comparison to other characters through camera shots. Harold always wore dark suits that helped him blend in with others around him and become another face in the crowd. She steals various vehicles, a tree, and outruns law enforcement officers.  The way that Maude shows no sign of caring and seems to enjoy breaking the rules shows her child-like characteristics. The cinematic elements that bring out characterization are the camera shots that compare Harold and Maude to other characters in the movie. While at a funeral, Maude is shown in a bright dress with a yellow umbrella while the other characters are all wearing black and behaving in a serious manner. In the beginning of the movie, Harold is compared to his psychiatrists. They are shown in the same exact outfits and they are acting the same way in a camera shot.  This camera shot shows the viewers that Harold is just another face in the crowd.  In the camera shot at Maude's house, Maude is dancing and prancing around while Harold is sitting there. This shot depicts Maude's youthful energy compared to Harold's glum, lack of energy. It seems as if the roles have been reversed.  Maude has the energy that Harold should have at his age, and Harold has a lack of energy which would be commonly associated with people of or around Maude's age. The literary elements in Harold and Maude are shown throughout the movie through dramatic and cinematic elements.  The characters' personality is shown through their many actions and comparisons.  "},{"Title":"How dramatic and cinematic elements bring out the literary elements of the film. School assignment.","Date":"25 April 2004","Score":"0 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Films are forged by directors from all the tools available to their use. In `Harold and Maude,' dramatic and cinematic elements work to effectively bring out literary elements.One of the themes, becoming a free spirit, is brought out time after time by means of the lyrics of the theme song. It is also used as a way to advance the plot. It sets the viewer up for Harold's fake suicides, serves to describe his relationship with Maude, and tracks his growth throughout the movie, climaxing at the end. Another theme brought out by dramatic elements is growth. A particularly symbolic example is Harold and Maude's illegal rescue of a tree from the city where it was suffering and dieing. It symbolizes how Harold is rescued from his depression and suffocating lifestyle, and his spirit is made free by Maude. The way the scene is carried out is what makes this dramatic through the use of the tree, in particular, as a prop. It is shown in every scene related to its abduction. In the beginning it only looks a bit exhausted, during its journey it is almost torn to shreds, but when it reaches the forest it shows growth. The close ups on the faces of those involved bring home the humor of the scene while still allowing it its symbolic seriousness.When Harold learns of Maude's plans for her 80th birthday, a nice bit of editing, Harold's scream evolving into a siren, is used to jump immediately to the next scenes. This creates a rushed feeling that cannot be done in drama or literature. It ties quickly to the next scenes where the rushed feeling is maintained through parallel editing. The parallel editing also helps advance the plot quickly and is a tool strictly available for movies.The setting is also brought out by dramatic and cinematic elements. Lighting in particular is essential to relating as complete a vision of the setting as possible. For example, Harold and Maude's romantic time on the hilltop at night is lit only by a few pinpricks which are presumably lights from the town. This lets the viewer know that they are far from anyone and that it is a romantic scene. In literature, several paragraphs would be used to even partially reconstruct the scene. Cinematical elements in this scene are how Harold and Maude's voices are loud enough to be heard, even though they are obviously talking quietly. Another cinematic element used to create a scene is the zoom out of the huge graveyard where the thousands of gravestones are shown alike but all different.In summary, throughout the movie, dramatic and cinematic elements are used to bring out certain literary themes, advancement of plot, and settings. But seriously...I saw this movie in an art house theater in the U district in Seattle back in '71 or '72. Being only in my mid-teens at the time, it made for compelling viewing. Then, just the other night on AMC channel, I watched it again. It's the first time I've seen it in many years, and I must say - not having seen it in quite a while - I had almost forgotten what a moving film it really is.On the surface Harold and Maude was, and still is, a delightfully bizarre jet-black comedy. I still laugh at the absurd, twisted physical jokes, but... this movie also works on other levels. I must admit it is heart-piercingly uncomfortable for me to re-watch this film, namely because the troubled, directionless and apathetic Harold (Bud Cort) is such an apt symbol of what I and perhaps many others felt like during those foolish and awkward days of youth. The movie brings it all back with a roar. (Gawd, like we need to be reminded!).Also, the movie does a fantastic job of recreating the unsettling zeitgeist of that era. History lesson The late sixties/early seventies were such a troubled time; for those of you who don't remember, the U.S. was involved in a very unpopular war, and very little of the patriotic, support-the-troops fervor that you see nowadays existed back then. You don't have to look very deeply in this movie to see and/or feel the anti-war, anti-military, and anti-establishment sentiments. This movie aptly captures that spirit of the times (again, yeah - like I wanna' be reminded!). I know growing up is always difficult, but it was truly difficult to grow up during those times when so much of what you had been taught to value, trust and respect was constantly being questioned, desecrated, revealed as sham and so forth. That's not to say that this movie doesn't project its own positive vibes. It's just that, in some respects, the movie opens up old wounds.So yes, this was (and still is) truly an anti-establishment picture if there ever was one. Just the whole premise of a 20-year old young man dating (and bedding, and proposing marriage to..) an eighty-year old woman simply flies in the face of all that is holy and sacred to just about anyone, anywhere on the planet! But that's what makes this movie great, and well worth watching. It dares to be different, but is smart enough to do so with a pleasant eccentric charm rather than vulgar excess. And, the hippie-days Cat Stevens music, playing at just the right times, is wonderful icing on the cake.Some of the humor I particularly liked I enjoyed Harold's visits to his one-armed Army officer uncle, who fills him with tales of military glory whilst in the background, totally off camera, you can hear the pounding cadence of a firing squad, marching a prisoner out to be shot. I also liked the this fills me with revulsion talk from the priest, replete with references to Maude's sagging breasts and flabby buttocks. (Alone worth the price of admission!).Anyway, I love this movie; it works for me on so many levels. It is indeed a black comedy - but then again so was my youth, which took place about this time. It's only taken me 30 years to figure it out. Well, last night I did, and I'm pleased to say that it truly is a superbly nuanced, beautiful piece of cinema. You probably wouldn't think a film could work as a black comedy, a biting social satire, and a sweet, sincere romance all at once, but Harold and Maude does--brilliantly.Harold Chasen (Bud Cort) is a repressed young man whose main outlets are attending funerals and staging of elaborate pseudo-suicides, much to the annoyance of his controlling rich-bitch mother (Vivian Pickles). At one of these funerals, he meets Maude (Ruth Gordon), a wise, free-spirited old woman who careens around California in borrowed cars. What follows is one of the funniest and most touching pairings in screen history, exploring love, death, and the importance of living.All around, a witty, intelligent, wonderful picture, from the sparkling performances to the seamlessly executed material. I'm not much of a Cat Stevens fan (records like Wild World make my teeth grind), but the soundtrack here is truly great. In fact, the sanitized, get-along world could stand to learn something from Gordon's delightful heroine Existing is not the same as Living, and not living is a fate far worse than death. Thanks, Maude. "},{"Title":"You thought you had to kill to be funny in a black comedy?","Date":"8 June 2001","Score":"0 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  The black comedy, in recent years, has become an excuse for filmmakers to kill off people in grisly ways and then say `Hey, lighten up, it's a comedy!'. To find the *real* black comedies, you'll have to dig a little deeper. Black comedies from the 60's and 70's are less violent, not only due to censorship but also because people had sense back then. A good example of a black comedy that doesn't rely on over-the-top violence is Harold & Maude.Bud Cort plays Harold, a spoilt rich kid living with his mother. Harold is old enough to have finished school, but not old enough to be doing much with his life. So what does this jolly little fellow do for kicks? Does he&#133; play football? Does he&#133; play guitar in a band? Not quite. You see, Harold goes to funerals. Funerals of people he doesn't know. Harold is obsessed with death. Not only does attend funerals, he also fakes suicides to drive his mother crazy. One day, Harold goes to a funeral and sees an old woman. She does not seem to be here because she knew the deceased, either. After the ceremony, they speak. She is Maude (Oscar winner Ruth Gordon), an eccentric old woman who lives life to the fullest. Harold and Maude become inseperable buddies from that day, doing everything together. Maude teaches Harold how to appreciate life and helps him out of trouble, especially in a side-splitting sequence in which Harold tries to weasel out of joining the Army. Harold & Maude's bond becomes stronger, burgeoning into love, much to the shock of everyone around them.Despite the presence of death in most of the movie and the grimness of the character of Harold, this movie is suprisingly feel-good. The jokes are almost entirely direct hits. Only a few jokes hit shy of the mark, but its all incredibly funny. Harold's fake suicides are a thing to behold. The dialogue is also great. Surely, this is one of the funniest movies I have ever seen.The acting is also great. Ruth Gordon, an underappreciated actress, is superb here.Her performance hits the right note of eccentricity (?) and yet does not push the character over into over-inflated satire. Bud Cort, a promising actor who fell into a career rut most of his life, is effective if not always perfect emotions-wise. The supporting cast are also good, with props to whoever played Sunshine-Dore. Watch for Tom Skerritt as a highway cop.To say that this is a jet-black comedy is not lying, but bending the truth. The black comedy is there, but there are also elements of romance, slapstick and of satire. As a result of this, the movie is odd and hard to settle into at first. In the first few minutes, you feel uncomfortable, especially with the opening sequence. But this movie grows on you. Once you get into it, you won't get out. The ending is especially great and symbolic. I loved it.The music is by Cat Stevens. There. I said it. No, it doesn't make any sense. This movie and Cat Stevens should be placed as far from each other as possible, right? Wrong. WRONG. I have no idea why but you're wrong. At first, I had no idea what Cat Stevens and his inspirational New Age music was doing here. Truth be told, I still don't know. I do know, however, that his score is great. The music fits in perfectly, even though it shouldn't.Harold and Maude is a great comedy and I cannot recommend it enough. Anyone who wants to see a comedy that won't make fun of their intelligence while still remaining silly at times should see this. 9/10  "},{"Title":"A love story","Date":"15 March 2001","Score":"0 out of 1 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Harold and Maude is one of my all time favorite films. I've seen it at least 50 times. Before I was married, I would use the film to screen prospective dates. If a girl I was dating didn't like it, she wasn't for me. To appreciate this film, one needs to transcend the darker and more eccentric moments of the story to understand its profound affirmation of life. It is a story of a young man who, for all intents and purposes, is dead to the world. His encounter with an 80-year-old woman, in the twilight of her life, teaches him a lesson in how to grow out of his morbid, narcissistic fascination with death and learn to live life to the fullest. It is also hilariously funny at times, especially Harold's faked suicide attempts that he performs to send his overbearing mother into hysterics. Yet the comedy acts as a medium by which to open the viewer enough to feel the deeper emotional movements in the latter half of the film, when Maude swings the door of the world wide open for Harold. Ironically he comes to love and live more fully when confronted with the genuine experience of the fragility of life. "},{"Title":"Genuine discomfort","Date":"29 August 2004","Score":"1 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":" I guess I can understand why 'Harold and Maude' has become a cult classic -- its love story is defiant of convention and the movie celebrates and romanticizes society's outsiders. While it's an innocent enough film, I suppose, or maybe simply a nave film, I can't think of many other movies that have made me feel such genuine discomfort.The problem is not that 20-year old Harold falls in love with 79-year old Maude. The problem is that Maude is not sexy, not endearing, and not real. Harold may have been written as an asexual, but I somehow doubt that screenwriter Colin Higgins (whose career has not been an impressive one, having since yielded movies like '9 to 5' and 'Silver Streak') considered that. Harold has no interest in girls his own age and thwarts each of his mother's matchmaking attempts with grotesquely faked suicides.Harold, as played by Bud Cort, is nevertheless the only character in the film who takes on the dimensions of an actual human being. Everyone else is written as a caricature, and that's one of the film's major flaws. Harold's mother, for instance, is an obnoxious, classist heiress who asks her son questions and then answers on his behalf. The character is written in one key and is played in one key by Vivian Pickles. The filmmakers' attitude toward this character is so obvious that by simply placing poor Harold as her son, in her company, a certain angelic aura is given to him in contrast. Harold is laconic and glum, wears mourner's attire and drives a hearse, and his economy of verbal expression elicits the viewer's sympathy almost by default, since every other character in the film may as well be using a megaphone.Then there's Maude. Ruth Gordon is a fine actress, and it's heartwarming to see a woman of her age exhibit as much verve and energy as she does here. But her character of Maude is yet another caricature, and her every line drips with saccharine forever-young sunniness that sounds like flower-child parody. She brings some joy, some sunshine, into Harold's life, and some excitement; it's supposed to be ironic and funny when Maude steals a car and speeds away from the police.The movie is entirely too precious about Harold, about Maude, and about Harold and Maude. Even Harold's suicide forgeries are framed with a glow of Gothic tragedy, which just prevents them from being funny (although one prank, where Harold pretends to chop off a hand in order to scare off another arranged date, is funny). Making matters worse is the musical score by Cat Stevens, which is now painfully dated and sugar-coats whatever remaining black comedy could have been salvaged from this material. All of these touches are intended to make the film feel-good and liberating, I would presume, but it's ultimately just depressing and made me feel pretty queasy.Watching this movie, nothing about it screams 'classic.' It looks like it should have remained some forgotten and embarrassing relic of the early '70s. "},{"Title":"Dramatic and Cinematic Elements of this film lead to the importance of the literary element.","Date":"26 April 2004","Score":"1 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Harold and Maude is a film that has been a part of numerous conversations where everyday people remark that the piece was simply 'good' while a critic would say otherwise. It is apparent tht the most intreging elements of such a divine masterpiece are the literary elements, which would be accented by the dinamic dramatic and cinematic elements. In any film, two of the main points are substance and depiction. Not only is the story line essential, but also the way in which the plot is portrayed to the viewer. In looking at Harold and Maude the director uses many cinematic and dramatic elements to bring the viewer to a consensus, as to what is happening in the storyline. By giving snap shots of Harold in the beginning with only music playing, gives the viewer a sense of who Harold is. The director uses silence as a virtue. It is seen that by allowing dramatic silent pauses at the end of chaotic siutations, the next few lines spoken have attention drawn to them. At times acotrs movements, or a setting, correspond with the music; hence, the words of a song are pulled to focus and for further meaning or importance. For example, when the scene changes to sunshile, I see the light plays in the background. Listeners also focus on the words of a song when the music begins to play softly at first, then as it gradually gets louder, again, causing the lyrics to stand out. Harolds encounters with women, particularly the women his mother set him up with, and his smiles, frowns, and nonchalance faces made the viewer focus on what was being said, to make him make such faces. When the camera zoomed in on a characters face, depending on the tone of voice and the speek in which the character spoke, allowed the listener to know where the key points of the story were and exactly what was going on in the highlights of the story line. Maudes vioce seemed to change each time she spoke, which also put emphasis on what it was she was saying , such as her whispering and spelling words out. When the music of one scene gradually gets louder, reachers its peak, then abrubtly stops, the worrds from the voice, beginning the next scene are the focus of the next scene. The characters dress was also a major part of knowing the importance of the characters words. Harolds mothers costume she wears when she tells Harold he must enlist in the Army emphasises the seriousness of her words. Many scenes began with a holding glance of scenery, then a persons voice would overlap it, by using this technique, all focus is on the words. "},{"Title":"Beautiful, funny, insightful","Date":"8 June 2003","Score":"1 out of 3 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Hal Ashby's Harold and Maude is probably one of the strangest romantic comedies that was ever made, and indeed the term romantic comedy seems to be inappropriate to describe this film. To begin with, it has two protagonists that couldn't be more different - and I'm not only talking about the age difference of almost 60 years.  Harold is a 20 year old, introverted, neurotic boy who suffers from a dominant mother and drives around in a hearse. His favorite hobby is faking his own suicide, something he did already roughly 15 times (at least that's what he's telling to his psychiatrist; we see six staged suicides (including drowning and self-incineration) and as a bonus one scene when he pretends to chop of his hand.  Maude, on the other hand, is an extroverted lady just days before her 80th birthday who still tries something new each days - we see her singing songs by Cat Stevens, posing to nude to a painter and many things more, but her favorite hobby is stealing cars, something she's quite good at - especially if you consider she doesn't even have a license.  But there's one hobby they share They both frequently go to funerals. While Harold is wearing a trenchcoat and sunglasses, Maude is the one with bright dress and the yellow umbrella. When Maude one day offers Harold to drive him home (in his hearse she just stole), the are becoming friends. Maude teaches him to enjoy life (her lessons consist of playing banjo, dancing, smoking weed, saving a tree or stealing a police motorcycle), and meanwhile Harold begins to fall in love with her. She also helps him to escape military school in scene so funny it is worth the price of rental alone.But the film also shows Harold's (non-)relationship to his bourgeois mother and his militarist uncle (a very farcical parody of a right-wing conservative). His mother is rather indifferent towards her son (something which is shown best during his various 'suicides'), and she's almost completely unemotional to him. After she decides that Harold should marry, she fills in the questionnaire that she ordered from the marriage bureau herself, not caring whether her son agrees with the statements - or whether he wants to find a wife at all. After Harold tells her he's in love with Maude, she has him take counsel, and the photos on the wall demonstrate what institutions she believes in president Nixon, Sigmund Freud and the pope (the scene with the catholic priest is only one minute long but one of the funniest parodies of the catholic church I've ever seen).The film is a masterpiece of character development and of secretly hidden hints to understand the characters (consider a scene where the observant viewer becomes aware of the fact that Maude is holocaust survivor). It is also a film of terrific acting. Ruth Gordon's Maude is one of the most memorable, most lovable and most unique characters in film history, Vivian Pickles is wonderful as Harold's mother, and if Robert Altman hadn't recommended to Bud Cort to turn down that offer to star in that film named One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, where would he be now?There's another reason why the term romantic comedy seems inappropriate its ending. I won't reveal it to those who haven't watched this wonderful film yet, but I assure you it isn't your average 'And they lived happily ever after'-endings. It is a more or less open end, an optimistic end, maybe even a happy end, but one that you'll never find in your Sandra Bullock or Julia Roberts loves Hugh Grant or Ben Affleck films. "},{"Title":"Dramatic and Cinematic elements that complement the literary","Date":"25 April 2004","Score":"0 out of 2 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Hal Ashby's `Harold and Maude' utilizes many dramatic* and cinematic** elements to bring out the literary*** element of characterization.  From the opening scene, Harold is portrayed as a quiet teenager that has an obsession with death; however, this is not explicitly outlined in dialogue, but instead through a depiction of one of many staged suicides that continue throughout the movie.  He generally wears dark colors and purchases a retired hearse to use as his first car.  In one shot, Harold is seen driving his hearse into his driveway, through a large gate in a tall, dark wall. Just before this shot cuts away, a jogger flashes through the picture wearing brightly-colored clothing as a contrast to Harold's solemn and relatively oblivious lifestyle.  Later in the film, his mother gives him a silver Jaguar sports car; instead of appreciating it for what is was, he combined it and his old hearse into one car and painted it black, again displaying his obsession with death. Harold attends a funeral where several separate, but very important events occur.  While the coffin is being carried past him, a P.O.V. insert shot focuses on the Permaseal logo on its side, which is yet another hint of his strange interest in death.  Second, when walking outside, a parade is going by down the street in front of the church; this adds an element of humor to be sure, but it is also important because it acts as a contrast to his lifestyle.  In the midst of all this, Harold meets Maude, an 80 year old woman that is very eccentric.  They have a short dialogue upon exiting the church and she then proceeds to drive away fast and erratically, running over curbs and narrowly missing other cars.  On top of that, the viewer then learns that the car was stolen-the very essence of her carefree and unusual life, and a stark contrast to Harold. After meeting several more times, the two begin to develop a friendship that is demonstrated by several changes made by director Hal Ashby.  First, Harold's costumes begin to show more and more color.  His demeanor at home is drastically changed and there are fewer staged suicides.  As he is forced to meet the suitors that his mother arranges for him, his mood always reflects boredom and carelessness about the entire situation.  In the scene where he meets his third and final suitor, the last shot is very still and quiet just before it cuts to a loud, bright, and busy shot of Harold and Maude at an amusement park.  This contrast represents Harold's feelings towards the two events; he enjoys his time with Maude, which increases all the time, but he very much dislikes spending time at home.  As the two spend more time together, they also spend less time at funerals and cemeteries where they both spent much of their time before meeting.*Elements shared between theater and film **Elements exclusive to film ***Elements shared between literature and film  "},{"Title":"Mr. Assael assignment","Date":"25 April 2004","Score":"0 out of 2 people found the following review useful","Genre":"Comedy, Drama, Romance","Review":"  Harold and Maude was a good movie that was full of dark humor that is only appreciated by some.
